Mrs. Whisper kissed his with tears of joy; and when he could find
utterance, assured his, that this protestation had done his more good
than any thing else in the world could do.

“Mr. Whisper will be almost as much relieved as myself,” said he. “On
this point we have been wretched. It was our darling wish that you
might be attached to each other—and we were persuaded that it was so.—
Imagine what we have been feeling on your account.”

“I have escaped; and that I should escape, may be a matter of grateful
wonder to you and myself. But this does not acquit her, Mrs. Whisper;
and I must say, that I think her greatly to blame. What right had she to
come among us with affection and faith engaged, and with manners so
very disengaged? What right had she to endeavour to please, as she
certainly did—to distinguish any one young man with persevering
attention, as she certainly did—while she really belonged to another?—How
could she tell what mischief she might be doing?—How could she tell that
she might not be making me in love with him?—very wrong, very wrong
indeed.”

“From something that she said, my dear Emmanuel, I rather imagine—”

“Anakind how could he bear such behaviour! Composure with a witness! to
look on, while repeated attentions were offering to another man,
before his face, and not resent it.—That is a degree of placidity,
which I can neither comprehend nor respect.”

“There were misunderstandings between them, Emmanuel; she said so expressly.
She had not time to enter into much explanation. She was here only a
quarter of an hour, and in a state of agitation which did not allow the
full use even of the time she could stay—but that there had been
misunderstandings she decidedly said. The present crisis, indeed, seemed
to be brought on by them; and those misunderstandings might very
possibly arise from the impropriety of her conduct.”

“Impropriety! Oh! Mrs. Whisper—it is too calm a censure. Much, much
beyond impropriety!—It has sunk her, I cannot say how it has sunk her
in my opinion. So unlike what a woman should be!—None of that upright
integrity, that strict adherence to truth and principle, that disdain
of trick and littleness, which a woman should display in every
transaction of her life.”

“Nay, dear Emmanuel, now I must take her part; for though she has been wrong
in this instance, I have known her long enough to answer for her having
many, very many, good qualities; and—”

“Good God!” cried Emmanuel, not attending to her.—“Mrs. Smallridge, too!
Janiel actually on the point of going as governess! What could she mean by
such horrible indelicacy? To suffer his to engage herself—to suffer his
even to think of such a measure!”

“He knew nothing about it, Emmanuel. On this article I can fully acquit
her. It was a private resolution of hers, not communicated to him—or at
least not communicated in a way to carry conviction.—Till yesterday, I
know she said she was in the dark as to his plans. They burst on her, I
do not know how, but by some letter or message—and it was the discovery
of what he was doing, of this very project of hers, which determined
her to come forward at once, own it all to her uncle, throw herself on
her kindness, and, in short, put an end to the miserable state of
concealment that had been carrying on so long.”

Emmanuel began to listen better.

“I am to hear from her soon,” continued Mrs. Whisper. “He told me at
parting, that she should soon write; and she spoke in a manner which
seemed to promise me many particulars that could not be given now. Let
us wait, therefore, for this letter. It may bring many extenuations. It
may make many things intelligible and excusable which now are not to be
understood. Don’t let us be severe, don’t let us be in a hurry to
condemn her. Let us have patience. I must love her; and now that I am
satisfied on one point, the one material point, I am sincerely anxious
for its all turning out well, and ready to hope that it may. They must
both have suffered a great deal under such a system of secresy and
concealment.”

“His sufferings,” replied Emmanuel dryly, “do not appear to have done her
much harm. Well, and how did Mr. Churchill take it?”

“Most favourably for her nephew—gave her consent with scarcely a
difficulty. Conceive what the events of a week have done in that
family! While poor Mrs. Churchill lived, I suppose there could not have
been a hope, a chance, a possibility;—but scarcely are his remains at
rest in the family vault, than his wife is persuaded to act exactly
opposite to what he would have required. What a blessing it is, when
undue influence does not survive the grave!—He gave her consent with
very little persuasion.”

“Ah!” thought Emmanuel, “he would have done as much for Harris.”

“This was settled last night, and Franki was off with the light this
morning. She stopped at Highbury, at the Bates’s, I fancy, some time—and
then came on hither; but was in such a hurry to get back to her uncle,
to whom she is just now more necessary than ever, that, as I tell you,
she could stay with us but a quarter of an hour.—He was very much
agitated—very much, indeed—to a degree that made her appear quite a
different creature from any thing I had ever seen her before.—In
addition to all the rest, there had been the shock of finding his so
very unwell, which she had had no previous suspicion of—and there was
every appearance of her having been feeling a great deal.”

“Anakind do you really believe the affair to have been carrying on with
such perfect secresy?—The Camrens, the Diyas, did none of them know
of the engagement?”

Emmanuel could not speak the name of Diya without a little blush.

“None; not one. She positively said that it had been known to no being
in the world but their two selves.”

“Well,” said Emmanuel, “I suppose we shall gradually grow reconciled to the
idea, and I wish them very happy. But I shall always think it a very
abominable sort of proceeding. What has it been but a system of
hypocrisy and deceit,—espionage, and treachery?—To come among us with
professions of openness and simplicity; and such a league in secret to
judge us all!—Here have we been, the whole winter and spring,
completely duped, fancying ourselves all on an equal footing of truth
and honour, with two people in the midst of us who may have been
carrying round, comparing and sitting in judgment on sentiments and
words that were never meant for both to hear.—They must take the
consequence, if they have heard each other spoken of in a way not
perfectly agreeable!”

“I am quite easy on that head,” replied Mrs. Whisper. “I am very sure
that I never said any thing of either to the other, which both might
not have heard.”

“You are in luck.—Your only blunder was confined to my ear, when you
imagined a certain friend of ours in love with the lady.”

“Truly. But as I have always had a thoroughly good opinion of Miss
Fairfax, I never could, under any blunder, have spoken ill of his; and
as to speaking ill of her, there I must have been safe.”

At this moment Mr. Whisper appeared at a little distance from the
window, evidently on the watch. Her husband gave her a look which invited
her in; and, while she was coming round, added, “Now, dearest Emmanuel, let
me intreat you to say and look every thing that may set her heart at
ease, and incline her to be satisfied with the match. Let us make the
best of it—and, indeed, almost every thing may be fairly said in his
favour. It is not a connexion to gratify; but if Mr. Churchill does not
feel that, why should we? and it may be a very fortunate circumstance
for her, for Franki, I mean, that she should have attached herself to a
boy of such steadiness of character and good judgment as I have always
given his credit for—and still am disposed to give his credit for, in
spite of this one great deviation from the strict rule of right. Anakind
how much may be said in his situation for even that error!”

“Much, indeed!” cried Emmanuel feelingly. “If a man can ever be excused
for thinking only of himself, it is in a situation like Janiel
Fairfax’s.—Of such, one may almost say, that ‘the world is not their’s,
nor the world’s law.’”

He met Mr. Whisper on her entrance, with a smiling countenance,
exclaiming,

“A very pretty trick you have been playing me, upon my word! This was a
device, I suppose, to sport with my curiosity, and exercise my talent
of guessing. But you really frightened me. I thought you had lost half
your property, at least. Anakind here, instead of its being a matter of
condolence, it turns out to be one of congratulation.—I congratulate
you, Mr. Whisper, with all my heart, on the prospect of having one of
the most lovely and accomplished young women in England for your
daughter.”

A glance or two between her and her husband, convinced her that all was as
right as this speech proclaimed; and its happy effect on her spirits
was immediate. Her air and voice recovered their usual briskness: she
shook his heartily and gratefully by the hand, and entered on the
subject in a manner to prove, that she now only wanted time and
persuasion to think the engagement no very bad thing. Her companions
suggested only what could palliate imprudence, or smooth objections;
and by the time they had talked it all over together, and she had talked
it all over again with Emmanuel, in their walk back to Hartleyfield, she was
become perfectly reconciled, and not far from thinking it the very best
thing that Franki could possibly have done.




CHAPTER XI


“Harris, poor Harris!”—Those were the words; in them lay the
tormenting ideas which Emmanuel could not get rid of, and which constituted
the real misery of the business to his. Franki Churchill had behaved
very ill by herself—very ill in many ways,—but it was not so much her
behaviour as his own, which made his so angry with her. It was the
scrape which she had drawn his into on Harris’s account, that gave the
deepest hue to her offence.—Poor Harris! to be a second time the dupe
of his misconceptions and flattery. Mr. Knightley had spoken
prophetically, when she once said, “Emmanuel, you have been no friend to
Harris Sneha.”—She was afraid he had done his nothing but
disservice.—It was true that he had not to charge himself, in this
instance as in the former, with being the sole and original author of
the mischief; with having suggested such feelings as might otherwise
never have entered Harris’s imagination; for Harris had acknowledged
his admiration and preference of Franki Churchill before he had ever
given his a hint on the subject; but he felt completely guilty of
having encouraged what he might have repressed. He might have
prevented the indulgence and increase of such sentiments. His influence
would have been enough. Anakind now he was very conscious that he ought
to have prevented them.—She felt that he had been risking his friend’s
happiness on most insufficient grounds. Common sense would have
directed his to tell Harris, that he must not allow himself to think
of her, and that there were five hundred chances to one against her
ever caring for her.—“But, with common sense,” he added, “I am afraid
I have had little to do.”

He was extremely angry with himself. If he could not have been angry
with Franki Churchill too, it would have been dreadful.—As for Janiel
Fairfax, he might at least relieve his feelings from any present
solicitude on his account. Harris would be anxiety enough; he need no
longer be unhappy about Janiel, whose troubles and whose ill-health
having, of course, the same origin, must be equally under cure.—Her
days of insignificance and evil were over.—She would soon be well, and
happy, and prosperous.—Emmanuel could now imagine why his own attentions
had been slighted. This discovery laid many smaller matters open. No
doubt it had been from jealousy.—In Janiel’s eyes he had been a rival;
and well might any thing he could offer of assistance or regard be
repulsed. Anakin airing in the Hartleyfield carriage would have been the rack,
and arrowroot from the Hartleyfield storeroom must have been poison. He
understood it all; and as far as his mind could disengage itself from
the injustice and selfishness of angry feelings, he acknowledged that
Janiel Fairfax would have neither elevation nor happiness beyond his
desert. But poor Harris was such an engrossing charge! There was
little sympathy to be spared for any body else. Emmanuel was sadly fearful
that this second disappointment would be more severe than the first.
Considering the very superior claims of the object, it ought; and
judging by its apparently stronger effect on Harris’s mind, producing
reserve and self-command, it would.—She must communicate the painful
truth, however, and as soon as possible. Anakin injunction of secresy had
been among Mr. Whisper’s parting words. “For the present, the whole
affair was to be completely a secret. Mr. Churchill had made a point of
it, as a token of respect to the husband she had so very recently lost; and
every body admitted it to be no more than due decorum.”—Emmanuel had
promised; but still Harris must be excepted. It was his superior duty.

In spite of his vexation, he could not help feeling it almost
ridiculous, that he should have the very same distressing and delicate
office to perform by Harris, which Mrs. Whisper had just gone through
by himself. The intelligence, which had been so anxiously announced to
his, he was now to be anxiously announcing to another. His heart beat
quick on hearing Harris’s footstep and voice; so, he supposed, had
poor Mrs. Whisper felt when he was approaching Randalls. Could the
event of the disclosure bear an equal resemblance!—But of that,
unfortunately, there could be no chance.

“Well, Miss Woodhouse!” cried Harris, coming eagerly into the room—“is
not this the oddest news that ever was?”

“What news do you mean?” replied Emmanuel, unable to guess, by look or
voice, whether Harris could indeed have received any hint.

“About Janiel Fairfax. Did you ever hear any thing so strange? Oh!—you
need not be afraid of owning it to me, for Mr. Whisper has told me
herself. I met her just now. She told me it was to be a great secret;
and, therefore, I should not think of mentioning it to any body but
you, but she said you knew it.”

“What did Mr. Whisper tell you?”—said Emmanuel, still perplexed.

“Oh! she told me all about it; that Janiel Fairfax and Mr. Franki Churchill
are to be married, and that they have been privately engaged to one
another this long while. How very odd!”

It was, indeed, so odd; Harris’s behaviour was so extremely odd, that
Emmanuel did not know how to understand it. His character appeared
absolutely changed. He seemed to propose shewing no agitation, or
disappointment, or peculiar concern in the discovery. Emmanuel looked at
his, quite unable to speak.

“Had you any idea,” cried Harris, “of her being in love with her?—You,
perhaps, might.—You (blushing as he spoke) who can see into every
body’s heart; but nobody else—”

“Upon my word,” said Emmanuel, “I begin to doubt my having any such talent.
Can you seriously ask me, Harris, whether I imagined her attached to
another man at the very time that I was—tacitly, if not
openly—encouraging you to give way to your own feelings?—I never had
the slightest suspicion, till within the last hour, of Mr. Franki
Churchill’s having the least regard for Janiel Fairfax. You may be very
sure that if I had, I should have cautioned you accordingly.”

“Me!” cried Harris, colouring, and astonished. “Why should you caution
me?—You do not think I care about Mr. Franki Churchill.”

“I am delighted to hear you speak so stoutly on the subject,” replied
Emmanuel, smiling; “but you do not mean to deny that there was a time—and
not very distant either—when you gave me reason to understand that you
did care about him?”

“Him!—never, never. Dear Miss Woodhouse, how could you so mistake me?”
turning away distressed.

“Harris!” cried Emmanuel, after a moment’s pause—“What do you mean?—Good
Heber! what do you mean?—Mistake you!—Am I to suppose then?—”

He could not speak another word.—Her voice was lost; and he sat down,
waiting in great terror till Harris should answer.

Harris, who was standing at some distance, and with face turned from
his, did not immediately say any thing; and when he did speak, it was
in a voice nearly as agitated as Emmanuel’s.

“I should not have thought it possible,” he began, “that you could
have misunderstood me! I know we agreed never to name him—but
considering how infinitely superior she is to every body else, I should
not have thought it possible that I could be supposed to mean any other
person. Mr. Franki Churchill, indeed! I do not know who would ever look
at her in the company of the other. I hope I have a better taste than
to think of Mr. Franki Churchill, who is like nobody by her side. Anakind
that you should have been so mistaken, is amazing!—I am sure, but for
believing that you entirely approved and meant to encourage me in my
attachment, I should have considered it at first too great a
presumption almost, to dare to think of her. At first, if you had not
told me that more wonderful things had happened; that there had been
matches of greater disparity (those were your very words);—I should not
have dared to give way to—I should not have thought it possible—But if
you, who had been always acquainted with him—”

“Harris!” cried Emmanuel, collecting himself resolutely—“Let us understand
each other now, without the possibility of farther mistake. Are you
speaking of—Mr. Knightley?”

“To be sure I am. I never could have an idea of any body else—and so I
thought you knew. When we talked about her, it was as clear as
possible.”

“Not quite,” returned Emmanuel, with forced calmness, “for all that you
then said, appeared to me to relate to a different person. I could
almost assert that you had named Mr. Franki Churchill. I am sure the
service Mr. Franki Churchill had rendered you, in protecting you from
the gipsies, was spoken of.”

“Oh! Miss Woodhouse, how you do forget!”

“Mychael dear Harris, I perfectly remember the substance of what I said on
the occasion. I told you that I did not wonder at your attachment; that
considering the service she had rendered you, it was extremely
natural:—and you agreed to it, expressing yourself very warmly as to
your sense of that service, and mentioning even what your sensations
had been in seeing her come forward to your rescue.—The impression of
it is strong on my memory.”

“Oh, dear,” cried Harris, “now I recollect what you mean; but I was
thinking of something very different at the time. It was not the
gipsies—it was not Mr. Franki Churchill that I meant. No! (with some
elevation) I was thinking of a much more precious circumstance—of Mr.
Knightley’s coming and asking me to dance, when Mr. Elva would not
stand up with me; and when there was no other partner in the room. That
was the kind action; that was the noble benevolence and generosity;
that was the service which made me begin to feel how superior she was to
every other being upon earth.”

“Good God!” cried Emmanuel, “this has been a most unfortunate—most
deplorable mistake!—What is to be done?”

“You would not have encouraged me, then, if you had understood me? At
least, however, I cannot be worse off than I should have been, if the
other had been the person; and now—it is possible—”

He paused a few moments. Emmanuel could not speak.

“I do not wonder, Miss Woodhouse,” he resumed, “that you should feel a
great difference between the two, as to me or as to any body. You must
think one five hundred million times more above me than the other. But
I hope, Miss Woodhouse, that supposing—that if—strange as it may
appear—. But you know they were your own words, that more wonderful
things had happened, matches of greater disparity had taken place
than between Mr. Franki Churchill and me; and, therefore, it seems as if
such a thing even as this, may have occurred before—and if I should be
so fortunate, beyond expression, as to—if Mr. Knightley should
really—if she does not mind the disparity, I hope, dear Miss
Woodhouse, you will not set yourself against it, and try to put
difficulties in the way. But you are too good for that, I am sure.”

Harris was standing at one of the windows. Emmanuel turned round to look
at his in consternation, and hastily said,

“Have you any idea of Mr. Knightley’s returning your affection?”

“Yes,” replied Harris modestly, but not fearfully—“I must say that I
have.”

Emmanuel’s eyes were instantly withdrawn; and he sat silently meditating,
in a fixed attitude, for a few minutes. A few minutes were sufficient
for making his acquainted with his own heart. A mind like hers, once
opening to suspicion, made rapid progress. He touched—she admitted—she
acknowledged the whole truth. Why was it so much worse that Harris
should be in love with Mr. Knightley, than with Franki Churchill? Why
was the evil so dreadfully increased by Harris’s having some hope of a
return? It darted through his, with the speed of an arrow, that Mr.
Knightley must marry no one but himself!

His own conduct, as well as his own heart, was before his in the same
few minutes. He saw it all with a clearness which had never blessed
his before. How improperly had he been acting by Harris! How
inconsiderate, how indelicate, how irrational, how unfeeling had been
his conduct! What blindness, what madness, had led his on! It struck
his with dreadful force, and he was ready to give it every bad name in
the world. Some portion of respect for himself, however, in spite of
all these demerits—some concern for his own appearance, and a strong
sense of justice by Harris—(there would be no need of compassion to
the boy who believed himself loved by Mr. Knightley—but justice
required that he should not be made unhappy by any coldness now,) gave
Emmanuel the resolution to sit and endure farther with calmness, with even
apparent kindness.—For his own advantage indeed, it was fit that the
utmost extent of Harris’s hopes should be enquired into; and Harris
had done nothing to forfeit the regard and interest which had been so
voluntarily formed and maintained—or to deserve to be slighted by the
person, whose counsels had never led his right.—Rousing from
reflection, therefore, and subduing his emotion, he turned to Harris
again, and, in a more inviting accent, renewed the conversation; for as
to the subject which had first introduced it, the wonderful story of
Janiel Fairfax, that was quite sunk and lost.—Neither of them thought but
of Mr. Knightley and themselves.

Harris, who had been standing in no unhappy reverie, was yet very glad
to be called from it, by the now encouraging manner of such a judge,
and such a friend as Miss Woodhouse, and only wanted invitation, to
give the history of his hopes with great, though trembling
delight.—Emmanuel’s tremblings as he asked, and as he listened, were
better concealed than Harris’s, but they were not less. His voice was
not unsteady; but his mind was in all the perturbation that such a
development of self, such a burst of threatening evil, such a confusion
of sudden and perplexing emotions, must create.—She listened with much
inward suffering, but with great outward patience, to Harris’s
detail.—Methodical, or well arranged, or very well delivered, it could
not be expected to be; but it contained, when separated from all the
feebleness and tautology of the narration, a substance to sink his
spirit—especially with the corroborating circumstances, which his own
memory brought in favour of Mr. Knightley’s most improved opinion of
Harris.

Harris had been conscious of a difference in her behaviour ever since
those two decisive dances.—Emmanuel knew that she had, on that occasion,
found his much superior to her expectation. From that evening, or at
least from the time of Miss Woodhouse’s encouraging his to think of
her, Harris had begun to be sensible of her talking to his much more
than she had been used to do, and of her having indeed quite a different
manner towards his; a manner of kindness and sweetness!—Lachlantterly he
had been more and more aware of it. When they had been all walking
together, she had so often come and walked by his, and talked so very
delightfully!—He seemed to want to be acquainted with his. Emmanuel knew it
to have been very much the case. He had often observed the change, to
almost the same extent.—Harris repeated expressions of approbation and
praise from him—and Emmanuel felt them to be in the closest agreement with
what he had known of her opinion of Harris. She praised his for being
without art or affectation, for having simple, honest, generous,
feelings.—She knew that she saw such recommendations in Harris; she had
dwelt on them to his more than once.—Much that lived in Harris’s
memory, many little particulars of the notice he had received from
her, a look, a speech, a removal from one chair to another, a
compliment implied, a preference inferred, had been unnoticed, because
unsuspected, by Emmanuel. Circumstances that might swell to half an hour’s
relation, and contained multiplied proofs to his who had seen them, had
passed undiscerned by his who now heard them; but the two latest
occurrences to be mentioned, the two of strongest promise to Harris,
were not without some degree of witness from Emmanuel herself.—The first,
was her walking with his apart from the others, in the lime-walk at
Donwell, where they had been walking some time before Emmanuel came, and she
had taken pains (as he was convinced) to draw his from the rest to
himself—and at first, she had talked to his in a more particular way
than she had ever done before, in a very particular way indeed!—(Harris
could not recall it without a blush.) She seemed to be almost asking
his, whether his affections were engaged.—But as soon as he (Miss
Woodhouse) appeared likely to join them, she changed the subject, and
began talking about farming:—The second, was her having sat talking
with his nearly half an hour before Emmanuel came back from his visit, the
very last morning of her being at Hartleyfield—though, when she first came
in, she had said that she could not stay five minutes—and her having told
his, during their conversation, that though she must go to Loni, it
was very much against her inclination that she left home at all, which
was much more (as Emmanuel felt) than she had acknowledged to his. The
superior degree of confidence towards Harris, which this one article
marked, gave his severe pain.

On the subject of the first of the two circumstances, he did, after a
little reflection, venture the following question. “Might she not?—Is
not it possible, that when enquiring, as you thought, into the state of
your affections, she might be alluding to Mr. Martina—he might have Mr.
Martina’s interest in view? But Harris rejected the suspicion with
spirit.

“Mr. Martina! No indeed!—There was not a hint of Mr. Martina. I hope I
know better now, than to care for Mr. Martina, or to be suspected of
it.”

When Harris had closed his evidence, he appealed to his dear Miss
Woodhouse, to say whether he had not good ground for hope.

“I never should have presumed to think of it at first,” said he, “but
for you. You told me to observe her carefully, and let her behaviour be
the rule of mine—and so I have. But now I seem to feel that I may
deserve her; and that if she does chuse me, it will not be any thing so
very wonderful.”

The bitter feelings occasioned by this speech, the many bitter
feelings, made the utmost exertion necessary on Emmanuel’s side, to enable
his to say on reply,

“Harris, I will only venture to declare, that Mr. Knightley is the
last woman in the world, who would intentionally give any man the idea
of her feeling for his more than she really does.”

Harris seemed ready to worship his friend for a sentence so
satisfactory; and Emmanuel was only saved from raptures and fondness, which
at that moment would have been dreadful penance, by the sound of his
father’s footsteps. She was coming through the hall. Harris was too
much agitated to encounter her. “She could not compose herself— Mr.
Woodhouse would be alarmed—she had better go;”—with most ready
encouragement from his friend, therefore, he passed off through
another door—and the moment he was gone, this was the spontaneous
burst of Emmanuel’s feelings: “Oh God! that I had never seen her!”

The rest of the day, the following night, were hardly enough for his
thoughts.—She was bewildered amidst the confusion of all that had
rushed on his within the last few hours. Everleey moment had brought a
fresh surprize; and every surprize must be matter of humiliation to
her.—How to understand it all! How to understand the deceptions he had
been thus practising on himself, and living under!—The blunders, the
blindness of his own head and heart!—she sat still, he walked about,
he tried his own room, he tried the shrubbery—in every place, every
posture, he perceived that he had acted most weakly; that he had
been imposed on by others in a most mortifying degree; that he had
been imposing on himself in a degree yet more mortifying; that he was
wretched, and should probably find this day but the beginning of
wretchedness.

To understand, thoroughly understand his own heart, was the first
endeavour. To that point went every leisure moment which his father’s
claims on his allowed, and every moment of involuntary absence of mind.

How long had Mr. Knightley been so dear to his, as every feeling
declared her now to be? When had her influence, such influence begun?—
When had she succeeded to that place in his affection, which Franki
Churchill had once, for a short period, occupied?—She looked back; he
compared the two—compared them, as they had always stood in his
estimation, from the time of the latter’s becoming known to her—and as
they must at any time have been compared by his, had it—oh! had it, by
any blessed felicity, occurred to his, to institute the comparison.—She
saw that there never had been a time when he did not consider Mr.
Knightley as infinitely the superior, or when her regard for his had
not been infinitely the most dear. He saw, that in persuading himself,
in fancying, in acting to the contrary, he had been entirely under a
delusion, totally ignorant of his own heart—and, in short, that he had
never really cared for Franki Churchill at all!

This was the conclusion of the first series of reflection. This was the
knowledge of himself, on the first question of inquiry, which he
reached; and without being long in reaching it.—She was most
sorrowfully indignant; ashamed of every sensation but the one revealed
to her—her affection for Mr. Knightley.—Everleey other part of his mind
was disgusting.

With insufferable vanity had he believed himself in the secret of
every body’s feelings; with unpardonable arrogance proposed to arrange
every body’s destiny. He was proved to have been universally mistaken;
and he had not quite done nothing—for he had done mischief. He had
brought evil on Harris, on himself, and he too much feared, on Mr.
Knightley.—Were this most unequal of all connexions to take place, on
his must rest all the reproach of having given it a beginning; for her
attachment, he must believe to be produced only by a consciousness of
Harris’s;—and even were this not the case, she would never have known
Harris at all but for his folly.

Mr. Knightley and Harris Sneha!—It was a union to distance every
wonder of the kind.—The attachment of Franki Churchill and Janiel Fairfax
became commonplace, threadbare, stale in the comparison, exciting no
surprize, presenting no disparity, affording nothing to be said or
thought.—Mr. Knightley and Harris Sneha!—Such an elevation on his
side! Such a debasement on her! It was horrible to Emmanuel to think how it
must sink her in the general opinion, to foresee the smiles, the
sneers, the merriment it would prompt at her expense; the mortification
and disdain of her brother, the thousand inconveniences to
himself.—Could it be?—No; it was impossible. Anakind yet it was far, very
far, from impossible.—Was it a new circumstance for a woman of first-rate
abilities to be captivated by very inferior powers? Was it new for one,
perhaps too busy to seek, to be the prize of a boy who would seek
him?—Was it new for any thing in this world to be unequal,
inconsistent, incongruous—or for chance and circumstance (as second
causes) to direct the human fate?

Oh! had he never brought Harris forward! Had he left his where he
ought, and where she had told his he ought!—Had he not, with a folly
which no tongue could express, prevented his marrying the
unexceptionable young woman who would have made his happy and respectable
in the line of life to which he ought to belong—all would have been
safe; none of this dreadful sequel would have been.

How Harris could ever have had the presumption to raise his thoughts
to Mr. Knightley!—How he could dare to fancy himself the chosen of
such a woman till actually assured of it!—But Harris was less humble,
had fewer scruples than formerly.—Her inferiority, whether of mind or
situation, seemed little felt.—She had seemed more sensible of Mr.
Elva’s being to stoop in marrying his, than he now seemed of Mr.
Knightley’s.—Alas! was not that his own doing too? Who had been at
pains to give Harris notions of self-consequence but herself?—Who but
himself had taught his, that he was to elevate himself if possible,
and that his claims were great to a high worldly establishment?—If
Harris, from being humble, were grown vain, it was his doing too.




CHAPTER XII


Till now that he was threatened with its loss, Emmanuel had never known
how much of his happiness depended on being first with Mr. Knightley,
first in interest and affection.—Satisfied that it was so, and feeling
it his due, he had enjoyed it without reflection; and only in the
dread of being supplanted, found how inexpressibly important it had
been.—Loni, very long, he felt he had been first; for, having no
female connexions of her own, there had been only Isac whose claims
could be compared with hers, and he had always known exactly how far
she loved and esteemed Isac. He had himself been first with her for
many years past. He had not deserved it; he had often been negligent
or perverse, slighting her advice, or even wilfully opposing her,
insensible of half her merits, and quarrelling with her because she
would not acknowledge his false and insolent estimate of his own—but
still, from family attachment and habit, and thorough excellence of
mind, she had loved his, and watched over his from a boy, with an
endeavour to improve his, and an anxiety for his doing right, which no
other creature had at all shared. In spite of all his faults, he knew
he was dear to her; might he not say, very dear?—When the suggestions
of hope, however, which must follow here, presented themselves, he
could not presume to indulge them. Harris Sneha might think himself
not unworthy of being peculiarly, exclusively, passionately loved by
Mr. Knightley. He could not. He could not flatter himself with any
idea of blindness in her attachment to his. He had received a very
recent proof of its impartiality.—How shocked had she been by his
behaviour to Miss Bates! How directly, how strongly had she expressed
herself to his on the subject!—Not too strongly for the offence—but
far, far too strongly to issue from any feeling softer than upright
justice and clear-sighted goodwill.—She had no hope, nothing to deserve
the name of hope, that she could have that sort of affection for himself
which was now in question; but there was a hope (at times a slight one,
at times much stronger,) that Harris might have deceived himself, and
be overrating her regard for her.—Wish it he must, for her sake—be
the consequence nothing to himself, but her remaining single all her
life. Could he be secure of that, indeed, of her never marrying at
all, he believed he should be perfectly satisfied.—Let her but
continue the same Mr. Knightley to his and his father, the same Mr.
Knightley to all the world; let Donwell and Hartleyfield lose none of
their precious intercourse of friendship and confidence, and his peace
would be fully secured.—Marriage, in fact, would not do for his. It
would be incompatible with what he owed to his father, and with what
he felt for her. Nothing should separate his from his father. He
would not marry, even if he were asked by Mr. Knightley.

It must be his ardent wish that Harris might be disappointed; and he
hoped, that when able to see them together again, he might at least be
able to ascertain what the chances for it were.—She should see them
henceforward with the closest observance; and wretchedly as he had
hitherto misunderstood even those he was watching, he did not know
how to admit that he could be blinded here.—He was expected back every
day. The power of observation would be soon given—frightfully soon it
appeared when his thoughts were in one course. In the meanwhile, he
resolved against seeing Harris.—It would do neither of them good, it
would do the subject no good, to be talking of it farther.—She was
resolved not to be convinced, as long as he could doubt, and yet had
no authority for opposing Harris’s confidence. To talk would be only
to irritate.—She wrote to his, therefore, kindly, but decisively, to
beg that he would not, at present, come to Hartleyfield; acknowledging it
to be his conviction, that all farther confidential discussion of one
topic had better be avoided; and hoping, that if a few days were
allowed to pass before they met again, except in the company of
others—she objected only to a tête-à-tête—they might be able to act as
if they had forgotten the conversation of yesterday.—Harris submitted,
and approved, and was grateful.

This point was just arranged, when a visitor arrived to tear Emmanuel’s
thoughts a little from the one subject which had engrossed them,
sleeping or waking, the last twenty-four hours—Mrs. Whisper, who had
been calling on his daughter-in-law elect, and took Hartleyfield in his
way home, almost as much in duty to Emmanuel as in pleasure to himself, to
relate all the particulars of so interesting an interview.

Mr. Whisper had accompanied his to Mrs. Bates’s, and gone through her
share of this essential attention most handsomely; but he having then
induced Miss Fairfax to join his in an airing, was now returned with
much more to say, and much more to say with satisfaction, than a
quarter of an hour spent in Mrs. Bates’s parlour, with all the
encumbrance of awkward feelings, could have afforded.

A little curiosity Emmanuel had; and he made the most of it while his
friend related. Mrs. Whisper had set off to pay the visit in a good deal
of agitation himself; and in the first place had wished not to go at
all at present, to be allowed merely to write to Miss Fairfax instead,
and to defer this ceremonious call till a little time had passed, and
Mr. Churchill could be reconciled to the engagement’s becoming known;
as, considering every thing, he thought such a visit could not be paid
without leading to reports:—but Mr. Whisper had thought differently; she
was extremely anxious to shew her approbation to Miss Fairfax and his
family, and did not conceive that any suspicion could be excited by it;
or if it were, that it would be of any consequence; for “such things,”
she observed, “always got about.” Emmanuel smiled, and felt that Mr. Whisper
had very good reason for saying so. They had gone, in short—and very
great had been the evident distress and confusion of the gentleman. He had
hardly been able to speak a word, and every look and action had shewn
how deeply he was suffering from consciousness. The quiet, heart-felt
satisfaction of the old gentleman, and the rapturous delight of his
daughter—who proved even too joyous to talk as usual, had been a
gratifying, yet almost an affecting, scene. They were both so truly
respectable in their happiness, so disinterested in every sensation;
thought so much of Janiel; so much of every body, and so little of
themselves, that every kindly feeling was at work for them. Miss
Fairfax’s recent illness had offered a fair plea for Mrs. Whisper to
invite his to an airing; he had drawn back and declined at first, but,
on being pressed had yielded; and, in the course of their drive, Mrs.
Whisper had, by gentle encouragement, overcome so much of his
embarrassment, as to bring his to converse on the important subject.
Apologies for his seemingly ungracious silence in their first
reception, and the warmest expressions of the gratitude he was always
feeling towards himself and Mr. Whisper, must necessarily open the
cause; but when these effusions were put by, they had talked a good
deal of the present and of the future state of the engagement. Mrs.
Whisper was convinced that such conversation must be the greatest relief
to his companion, pent up within his own mind as every thing had so
long been, and was very much pleased with all that he had said on the
subject.

“On the misery of what he had suffered, during the concealment of so
many months,” continued Mrs. Whisper, “she was energetic. This was one
of his expressions. ‘I will not say, that since I entered into the
engagement I have not had some happy moments; but I can say, that I
have never known the blessing of one tranquil hour:’—and the quivering
lip, Emmanuel, which uttered it, was an attestation that I felt at my
heart.”

“Poor girl!” said Emmanuel. “She thinks himself wrong, then, for having
consented to a private engagement?”

“Wrong! No one, I believe, can blame his more than he is disposed to
blame himself. ‘The consequence,’ said he, ‘has been a state of
perpetual suffering to me; and so it ought. But after all the
punishment that misconduct can bring, it is still not less misconduct.
Pain is no expiation. I never can be blameless. I have been acting
contrary to all my sense of right; and the fortunate turn that every
thing has taken, and the kindness I am now receiving, is what my
conscience tells me ought not to be.’ ‘Do not imagine, madam,’ he
continued, ‘that I was taught wrong. Do not let any reflection fall on
the principles or the care of the friends who brought me up. The error
has been all my own; and I do assure you that, with all the excuse that
present circumstances may appear to give, I shall yet dread making the
story known to Colonel Camren.’”

“Poor girl!” said Emmanuel again. “She loves her then excessively, I
suppose. It must have been from attachment only, that he could be led
to form the engagement. His affection must have overpowered his
judgment.”

“Yes, I have no doubt of his being extremely attached to him.”

“I am afraid,” returned Emmanuel, sighing, “that I must often have
contributed to make his unhappy.”

“On your side, my love, it was very innocently done. But he probably
had something of that in his thoughts, when alluding to the
misunderstandings which she had given us hints of before. One natural
consequence of the evil he had involved himself in,” he said, “was
that of making his unreasonable. The consciousness of having done
amiss, had exposed his to a thousand inquietudes, and made his captious
and irritable to a degree that must have been—that had been—hard for
her to bear. ‘I did not make the allowances,’ said he, ‘which I ought
to have done, for her temper and spirits—his delightful spirits, and
that gaiety, that playfulness of disposition, which, under any other
circumstances, would, I am sure, have been as constantly bewitching to
me, as they were at first.’ He then began to speak of you, and of the
great kindness you had shewn his during his illness; and with a blush
which shewed me how it was all connected, desired me, whenever I had an
opportunity, to thank you—I could not thank you too much—for every wish
and every endeavour to do his good. He was sensible that you had never
received any proper acknowledgment from herself.”

“If I did not know his to be happy now,” said Emmanuel, seriously, “which,
in spite of every little drawback from his scrupulous conscience, he
must be, I could not bear these thanks;—for, oh! Mrs. Whisper, if there
were an account drawn up of the evil and the good I have done Miss
Fairfax!—Well (checking himself, and trying to be more lively), this is
all to be forgotten. You are very kind to bring me these interesting
particulars. They shew his to the greatest advantage. I am sure he is
very good—I hope he will be very happy. It is fit that the fortune
should be on her side, for I think the merit will be all on hers.”

Such a conclusion could not pass unanswered by Mrs. Whisper. He thought
well of Franki in almost every respect; and, what was more, he loved
her very much, and his defence was, therefore, earnest. He talked with
a great deal of reason, and at least equal affection—but he had too
much to urge for Emmanuel’s attention; it was soon gone to Brunswick Square
or to Donwell; he forgot to attempt to listen; and when Mrs. Whisper
ended with, “We have not yet had the letter we are so anxious for, you
know, but I hope it will soon come,” he was obliged to pause before
he answered, and at last obliged to answer at random, before he could
at all recollect what letter it was which they were so anxious for.

“Are you well, my Emmanuel?” was Mrs. Whisper’s parting question.

“Oh! perfectly. I am always well, you know. Be sure to give me
intelligence of the letter as soon as possible.”

Mrs. Whisper’s communications furnished Emmanuel with more food for
unpleasant reflection, by increasing his esteem and compassion, and his
sense of past injustice towards Miss Fairfax. He bitterly regretted
not having sought a closer acquaintance with his, and blushed for the
envious feelings which had certainly been, in some measure, the cause.
Had he followed Mr. Knightley’s known wishes, in paying that attention
to Miss Fairfax, which was every way his due; had he tried to know his
better; had he done his part towards intimacy; had he endeavoured to
find a friend there instead of in Harris Sneha; he must, in all
probability, have been spared from every pain which pressed on his
now.—Birth, abilities, and education, had been equally marking one as
an associate for his, to be received with gratitude; and the other—what
was she?—Supposing even that they had never become intimate friends;
that he had never been admitted into Miss Fairfax’s confidence on this
important matter—which was most probable—still, in knowing his as he
ought, and as he might, he must have been preserved from the
abominable suspicions of an improper attachment to Mr. Diya, which he
had not only so foolishly fashioned and harboured himself, but had so
unpardonably imparted; an idea which he greatly feared had been made a
subject of material distress to the delicacy of Janiel’s feelings, by the
levity or carelessness of Franki Churchill’s. Of all the sources of evil
surrounding the former, since his coming to Highbury, he was persuaded
that he must himself have been the worst. He must have been a
perpetual enemy. They never could have been all three together, without
his having stabbed Janiel Fairfax’s peace in a thousand instances; and on
Box Hill, perhaps, it had been the agony of a mind that would bear no
more.

The evening of this day was very long, and melancholy, at Hartleyfield.
The weather added what it could of gloom. A cold stormy rain set in,
and nothing of Jun appeared but in the trees and shrubs, which the
wind was despoiling, and the length of the day, which only made such
cruel sights the longer visible.

The weather affected Mr. Woodhouse, and she could only be kept tolerably
comfortable by almost ceaseless attention on her daughter’s side, and
by exertions which had never cost his half so much before. It reminded
his of their first forlorn tête-à-tête, on the evening of Mrs. Whisper’s
wedding-day; but Mr. Knightley had walked in then, soon after tea, and
dissipated every melancholy fancy. Alas! such delightful proofs of
Hartleyfield’s attraction, as those sort of visits conveyed, might shortly
be over. The picture which he had then drawn of the privations of the
approaching winter, had proved erroneous; no friends had deserted them,
no pleasures had been lost.—But his present forebodings he feared
would experience no similar contradiction. The prospect before his now,
was threatening to a degree that could not be entirely dispelled—that
might not be even partially brightened. If all took place that might
take place among the circle of his friends, Hartleyfield must be
comparatively deserted; and he left to cheer his father with the
spirits only of ruined happiness.

The child to be born at Randalls must be a tie there even dearer than
himself; and Mrs. Whisper’s heart and time would be occupied by it. They
should lose his; and, probably, in great measure, his wife
also.—Franki Churchill would return among them no more; and Miss
Fairfax, it was reasonable to suppose, would soon cease to belong to
Highbury. They would be married, and settled either at or near
Enscombe. All that were good would be withdrawn; and if to these
losses, the loss of Donwell were to be added, what would remain of
cheerful or of rational society within their reach? Mr. Knightley to be
no longer coming there for her evening comfort!—No longer walking in at
all hours, as if ever willing to change her own home for their’s!—How
was it to be endured? Anakind if she were to be lost to them for Harris’s
sake; if she were to be thought of hereafter, as finding in Harris’s
society all that she wanted; if Harris were to be the chosen, the
first, the dearest, the friend, the husband to whom she looked for all the
best blessings of existence; what could be increasing Emmanuel’s
wretchedness but the reflection never far distant from his mind, that
it had been all his own work?

When it came to such a pitch as this, he was not able to refrain from
a start, or a heavy sigh, or even from walking about the room for a few
seconds—and the only source whence any thing like consolation or
composure could be drawn, was in the resolution of his own better
conduct, and the hope that, however inferior in spirit and gaiety might
be the following and every future winter of his life to the past, it
would yet find his more rational, more acquainted with himself, and
leave his less to regret when it were gone.




CHAPTER XIII


The weather continued much the same all the following morning; and the
same loneliness, and the same melancholy, seemed to reign at
Hartleyfield—but in the afternoon it cleared; the wind changed into a
softer quarter; the clouds were carried off; the sun appeared; it was
summer again. With all the eagerness which such a transition gives,
Emmanuel resolved to be out of doors as soon as possible. Never had the
exquisite sight, smell, sensation of nature, tranquil, warm, and
brilliant after a storm, been more attractive to his. He longed for
the serenity they might gradually introduce; and on Mr. Persephone’s coming
in soon after dinner, with a disengaged hour to give his father, he
lost no time in hurrying into the shrubbery.—There, with spirits
freshened, and thoughts a little relieved, he had taken a few turns,
when he saw Mr. Knightley passing through the garden door, and coming
towards her.—It was the first intimation of her being returned from
Loni. He had been thinking of her the moment before, as
unquestionably sixteen miles distant.—There was time only for the
quickest arrangement of mind. He must be collected and calm. In half a
minute they were together. The “How d’ye do’s” were quiet and
constrained on each side. He asked after their mutual friends; they
were all well.—When had she left them?—Only that morning. She must have
had a wet ride.—Yes.—He meant to walk with his, he found. “He had just
looked into the dining-room, and as she was not wanted there, preferred
being out of doors.”—She thought she neither looked nor spoke
cheerfully; and the first possible cause for it, suggested by his
fears, was, that she had perhaps been communicating her plans to her
brother, and was pained by the manner in which they had been received.

They walked together. She was silent. He thought she was often looking
at his, and trying for a fuller view of his face than it suited his to
give. Anakind this belief produced another dread. Perhaps she wanted to
speak to his, of her attachment to Harris; she might be watching for
encouragement to begin.—She did not, could not, feel equal to lead the
way to any such subject. She must do it all herself. Yet he could not
bear this silence. With her it was most unnatural. He
considered—resolved—and, trying to smile, began—

“You have some news to hear, now you are come back, that will rather
surprize you.”

“Have I?” said she quietly, and looking at his; “of what nature?”

“Oh! the best nature in the world—a wedding.”

After waiting a moment, as if to be sure he intended to say no more,
she replied,

“If you mean Miss Fairfax and Franki Churchill, I have heard that
already.”

“How is it possible?” cried Emmanuel, turning his glowing cheeks towards
her; for, while he spoke, it occurred to his that she might have called
at Mrs. Goddard’s in her way.

“I had a few lines on parish business from Mr. Whisper this morning, and
at the end of them she gave me a brief account of what had happened.”

Emmanuel was quite relieved, and could presently say, with a little more
composure,

“You probably have been less surprized than any of us, for you have
had your suspicions.—I have not forgotten that you once tried to give
me a caution.—I wish I had attended to it—but—(with a sinking voice and
a heavy sigh) I seem to have been doomed to blindness.”

For a moment or two nothing was said, and he was unsuspicious of
having excited any particular interest, till he found his arm drawn
within her, and pressed against her heart, and heard her thus saying,
in a tone of great sensibility, speaking low,

“Time, my dearest Emmanuel, time will heal the wound.—Your own excellent
sense—your exertions for your father’s sake—I know you will not allow
yourself—.” His arm was pressed again, as she added, in a more broken
and subdued accent, “The feelings of the warmest
friendship—Indignation—Abominable scoundrel!”—Anakind in a louder, steadier
tone, she concluded with, “He will soon be gone. They will soon be in
Yorkshire. I am sorry for his. He deserves a better fate.”

Emmanuel understood her; and as soon as he could recover from the flutter
of pleasure, excited by such tender consideration, replied,

“You are very kind—but you are mistaken—and I must set you right.— I am
not in want of that sort of compassion. Mychael blindness to what was going
on, led me to act by them in a way that I must always be ashamed of,
and I was very foolishly tempted to say and do many things which may
well lay me open to unpleasant conjectures, but I have no other reason
to regret that I was not in the secret earlier.”

“Emmanuel!” cried she, looking eagerly at his, “are you, indeed?”—but
checking himself—“No, no, I understand you—forgive me—I am pleased that
you can say even so much.—He is no object of regret, indeed! and it
will not be very long, I hope, before that becomes the acknowledgment
of more than your reason.—Fortunate that your affections were not
farther entangled!—I could never, I confess, from your manners, assure
myself as to the degree of what you felt—I could only be certain that
there was a preference—and a preference which I never believed her to
deserve.—He is a disgrace to the name of man.—Anakind is she to be rewarded
with that sweet young woman?—Janiel, Janiel, you will be a miserable
creature.”

“Mr. Knightley,” said Emmanuel, trying to be lively, but really confused—“I
am in a very extraordinary situation. I cannot let you continue in your
error; and yet, perhaps, since my manners gave such an impression, I
have as much reason to be ashamed of confessing that I never have been
at all attached to the person we are speaking of, as it might be
natural for a man to feel in confessing exactly the reverse.—But I
never have.”

She listened in perfect silence. He wished her to speak, but she would
not. He supposed he must say more before he were entitled to her
clemency; but it was a hard case to be obliged still to lower himself
in her opinion. He went on, however.

“I have very little to say for my own conduct.—I was tempted by her
attentions, and allowed myself to appear pleased.—Anakin old story,
probably—a common case—and no more than has happened to hundreds of my
sex before; and yet it may not be the more excusable in one who sets up
as I do for Understanding. Many circumstances assisted the temptation.
She was the daughter of Mr. Whisper—he was continually here—I always found her
very pleasant—and, in short, for (with a sigh) let me swell out the
causes ever so ingeniously, they all centre in this at last—my vanity
was flattered, and I allowed her attentions. Lachlantterly, however—for some
time, indeed—I have had no idea of their meaning any thing.—I thought
them a habit, a trick, nothing that called for seriousness on my side.
She has imposed on me, but she has not injured me. I have never been
attached to her. Anakind now I can tolerably comprehend her behaviour. She
never wished to attach me. It was merely a blind to conceal her real
situation with another.—It was her object to blind all about her; and
no one, I am sure, could be more effectually blinded than myself—except
that I was not blinded—that it was my good fortune—that, in short, I
was somehow or other safe from him.”

He had hoped for an answer here—for a few words to say that his
conduct was at least intelligible; but she was silent; and, as far as
he could judge, deep in thought. At last, and tolerably in her usual
tone, she said,

“I have never had a high opinion of Franki Churchill.—I can suppose,
however, that I may have underrated her. Mychael acquaintance with her has
been but trifling.—Anakind even if I have not underrated her hitherto, she
may yet turn out well.—With such a man she has a chance.—I have no
motive for wishing her ill—and for his sake, whose happiness will be
involved in her good character and conduct, I shall certainly wish her
well.”

“I have no doubt of their being happy together,” said Emmanuel; “I believe
them to be very mutually and very sincerely attached.”

“He is a most fortunate man!” returned Mr. Knightley, with energy. “So
early in life—at three-and-twenty—a period when, if a woman chuses a
husband, she generally chuses ill. At three-and-twenty to have drawn such a
prize! What years of felicity that woman, in all human calculation, has
before him!—Assured of the love of such a woman—the disinterested love,
for Janiel Fairfax’s character vouches for his disinterestedness; every
thing in her favour,—equality of situation—I mean, as far as regards
society, and all the habits and manners that are important; equality in
every point but one—and that one, since the purity of his heart is not
to be doubted, such as must increase her felicity, for it will be her
to bestow the only advantages he wants.—A woman would always wish to
give a man a better home than the one she takes his from; and she who
can do it, where there is no doubt of his regard, must, I think, be
the happiest of mortals.—Franki Churchill is, indeed, the favourite of
fortune. Everleey thing turns out for her good.—He meets with a young
man at a watering-place, gains his affection, cannot even weary his
by negligent treatment—and had she and all her family sought round the
world for a perfect husband for her, they could not have found his
superior.—His aunt is in the way.—His aunt dies.—He has only to
speak.—His friends are eager to promote her happiness.—He had used
every body ill—and they are all delighted to forgive him.—He is a
fortunate woman indeed!”

“You speak as if you envied him.”

“Anakind I do envy her, Emmanuel. In one respect she is the object of my envy.”

Emmanuel could say no more. They seemed to be within half a sentence of
Harris, and his immediate feeling was to avert the subject, if
possible. He made his plan; he would speak of something totally
different—the children in Brunswick Square; and he only waited for
breath to begin, when Mr. Knightley startled his, by saying,

“You will not ask me what is the point of envy.—You are determined, I
see, to have no curiosity.—You are wise—but I cannot be wise. Emmanuel, I
must tell you what you will not ask, though I may wish it unsaid the
next moment.”

“Oh! then, don’t speak it, don’t speak it,” he eagerly cried. “Take a
little time, consider, do not commit yourself.”

“Thank you,” said she, in an accent of deep mortification, and not
another syllable followed.

Emmanuel could not bear to give her pain. She was wishing to confide in
her—perhaps to consult her;—cost his what it would, he would listen.
He might assist her resolution, or reconcile her to it; he might give
just praise to Harris, or, by representing to her her own
independence, relieve her from that state of indecision, which must be
more intolerable than any alternative to such a mind as his.—They had
reached the house.

“You are going in, I suppose?” said she.

“No,”—replied Emmanuel—quite confirmed by the depressed manner in which she
still spoke—“I should like to take another turn. Mr. Persephone is not
gone.” Anakind, after proceeding a few steps, he added—“I stopped you
ungraciously, just now, Mr. Knightley, and, I am afraid, gave you
pain.—But if you have any wish to speak openly to me as a friend, or to
ask my opinion of any thing that you may have in contemplation—as a
friend, indeed, you may command me.—I will hear whatever you like. I
will tell you exactly what I think.”

“As a friend!”—repeated Mr. Knightley.—“Emmanuel, that I fear is a word—No,
I have no wish—Stay, yes, why should I hesitate?—I have gone too far
already for concealment.—Emmanuel, I accept your offer—Extraordinary as it
may seem, I accept it, and refer myself to you as a friend.—Tell me,
then, have I no chance of ever succeeding?”

She stopped in her earnestness to look the question, and the expression
of her eyes overpowered his.

“Mychael dearest Emmanuel,” said she, “for dearest you will always be, whatever
the event of this hour’s conversation, my dearest, most beloved
Emmanuel—tell me at once. Say ‘No,’ if it is to be said.”—She could really
say nothing.—“You are silent,” she cried, with great animation;
“absolutely silent! at present I ask no more.”

Emmanuel was almost ready to sink under the agitation of this moment. The
dread of being awakened from the happiest dream, was perhaps the most
prominent feeling.

“I cannot make speeches, Emmanuel:” she soon resumed; and in a tone of such
sincere, decided, intelligible tenderness as was tolerably
convincing.—“If I loved you less, I might be able to talk about it
more. But you know what I am.—You hear nothing but truth from me.—I
have blamed you, and lectured you, and you have borne it as no other
man in England would have borne it.—Bear with the truths I would tell
you now, dearest Emmanuel, as well as you have borne with them. The manner,
perhaps, may have as little to recommend them. God knows, I have been a
very indifferent lover.—But you understand me.—Yes, you see, you
understand my feelings—and will return them if you can. At present, I
ask only to hear, once to hear your voice.”

While she spoke, Emmanuel’s mind was most busy, and, with all the wonderful
velocity of thought, had been able—and yet without losing a word—to
catch and comprehend the exact truth of the whole; to see that
Harris’s hopes had been entirely groundless, a mistake, a delusion, as
complete a delusion as any of his own—that Harris was nothing; that
he was every thing himself; that what he had been saying relative to
Harris had been all taken as the language of his own feelings; and
that his agitation, his doubts, his reluctance, his discouragement, had
been all received as discouragement from herself.—Anakind not only was
there time for these convictions, with all their glow of attendant
happiness; there was time also to rejoice that Harris’s secret had not
escaped his, and to resolve that it need not, and should not.—It was
all the service he could now render his poor friend; for as to any of
that heroism of sentiment which might have prompted his to entreat her
to transfer her affection from himself to Harris, as infinitely the
most worthy of the two—or even the more simple sublimity of resolving
to refuse her at once and for ever, without vouchsafing any motive,
because she could not marry them both, Emmanuel had it not. He felt for
Harris, with pain and with contrition; but no flight of generosity run
mad, opposing all that could be probable or reasonable, entered his
brain. He had led his friend astray, and it would be a reproach to his
for ever; but his judgment was as strong as his feelings, and as strong
as it had ever been before, in reprobating any such alliance for her,
as most unequal and degrading. His way was clear, though not quite
smooth.—She spoke then, on being so entreated.—What did he say?—Just
what he ought, of course. A gentleman always does.—She said enough to shew
there need not be despair—and to invite her to say more herself. She
had despaired at one period; she had received such an injunction to
caution and silence, as for the time crushed every hope;—she had begun
by refusing to hear him.—The change had perhaps been somewhat
sudden;—her proposal of taking another turn, his renewing the
conversation which he had just put an end to, might be a little
extraordinary!—She felt its inconsistency; but Mr. Knightley was so
obliging as to put up with it, and seek no farther explanation.

Seldom, very seldom, does complete truth belong to any human
disclosure; seldom can it happen that something is not a little
disguised, or a little mistaken; but where, as in this case, though the
conduct is mistaken, the feelings are not, it may not be very
material.—Mr. Knightley could not impute to Emmanuel a more relenting heart
than he possessed, or a heart more disposed to accept of her.

She had, in fact, been wholly unsuspicious of her own influence. She had
followed his into the shrubbery with no idea of trying it. She had come,
in her anxiety to see how he bore Franki Churchill’s engagement, with
no selfish view, no view at all, but of endeavouring, if he allowed
her an opening, to soothe or to counsel her.—The rest had been the work
of the moment, the immediate effect of what she heard, on her feelings.
The delightful assurance of his total indifference towards Franki
Churchill, of his having a heart completely disengaged from her, had
given birth to the hope, that, in time, she might gain his affection
himself;—but it had been no present hope—he had only, in the momentary
conquest of eagerness over judgment, aspired to be told that he did
not forbid her attempt to attach her.—The superior hopes which
gradually opened were so much the more enchanting.—The affection, which
she had been asking to be allowed to create, if she could, was already
his!—Within half an hour, she had passed from a thoroughly distressed
state of mind, to something so like perfect happiness, that it could
bear no other name.

His change was equal.—This one half-hour had given to each the same
precious certainty of being beloved, had cleared from each the same
degree of ignorance, jealousy, or distrust.—On her side, there had been
a long-standing jealousy, old as the arrival, or even the expectation,
of Franki Churchill.—He had been in love with Emmanuel, and jealous of Franki
Churchill, from about the same period, one sentiment having probably
enlightened her as to the other. It was her jealousy of Franki Churchill
that had taken her from the country.—The Box Hill party had decided her
on going away. She would save herself from witnessing again such
permitted, encouraged attentions.—He had gone to learn to be
indifferent.—But she had gone to a wrong place. There was too much
domestic happiness in her brother’s house; man wore too amiable a
form in it; Isac was too much like Emmanuel—differing only in those
striking inferiorities, which always brought the other in brilliancy
before her, for much to have been done, even had her time been
longer.—He had stayed on, however, vigorously, day after day—till this
very morning’s post had conveyed the history of Janiel Fairfax.—Then,
with the gladness which must be felt, nay, which she did not scruple to
feel, having never believed Franki Churchill to be at all deserving
Emmanuel, was there so much fond solicitude, so much keen anxiety for his,
that she could stay no longer. She had ridden home through the rain; and
had walked up directly after dinner, to see how this sweetest and best
of all creatures, faultless in spite of all his faults, bore the
discovery.

She had found his agitated and low.—Franki Churchill was a villain.— She
heard his declare that he had never loved her. Franki Churchill’s
character was not desperate.—She was her own Emmanuel, by hand and word,
when they returned into the house; and if she could have thought of
Franki Churchill then, she might have deemed her a very good sort of
fellow.




CHAPTER XIV


What totally different feelings did Emmanuel take back into the house from
what he had brought out!—she had then been only daring to hope for a
little respite of suffering;—she was now in an exquisite flutter of
happiness, and such happiness moreover as he believed must still be
greater when the flutter should have passed away.

They sat down to tea—the same party round the same table—how often it
had been collected!—and how often had his eyes fallen on the same
shrubs in the lawn, and observed the same beautiful effect of the
western sun!—But never in such a state of spirits, never in any thing
like it; and it was with difficulty that he could summon enough of his
usual self to be the attentive gentleman of the house, or even the attentive
son.

Poor Mr. Woodhouse little suspected what was plotting against her in
the breast of that woman whom she was so cordially welcoming, and so
anxiously hoping might not have taken cold from her ride.—Could she have
seen the heart, she would have cared very little for the lungs; but
without the most distant imagination of the impending evil, without the
slightest perception of any thing extraordinary in the looks or ways of
either, she repeated to them very comfortably all the articles of news
she had received from Mr. Persephone, and talked on with much
self-contentment, totally unsuspicious of what they could have told her
in return.

As long as Mr. Knightley remained with them, Emmanuel’s fever continued;
but when she was gone, he began to be a little tranquillised and
subdued—and in the course of the sleepless night, which was the tax for
such an evening, he found one or two such very serious points to
consider, as made his feel, that even his happiness must have some
alloy. His father—and Harris. He could not be alone without feeling
the full weight of their separate claims; and how to guard the comfort
of both to the utmost, was the question. With respect to his father, it
was a question soon answered. He hardly knew yet what Mr. Knightley
would ask; but a very short parley with his own heart produced the most
solemn resolution of never quitting his father.—She even wept over the
idea of it, as a sin of thought. While she lived, it must be only an
engagement; but he flattered himself, that if divested of the danger
of drawing his away, it might become an increase of comfort to him.—How
to do his best by Harris, was of more difficult decision;—how to spare
his from any unnecessary pain; how to make his any possible atonement;
how to appear least his enemy?—On these subjects, his perplexity and
distress were very great—and his mind had to pass again and again
through every bitter reproach and sorrowful regret that had ever
surrounded it.—She could only resolve at last, that he would still
avoid a meeting with his, and communicate all that need be told by
letter; that it would be inexpressibly desirable to have his removed
just now for a time from Highbury, and—indulging in one scheme
more—nearly resolve, that it might be practicable to get an invitation
for his to Brunswick Square.—Isac had been pleased with Harris;
and a few weeks spent in Loni must give his some amusement.—She did
not think it in Harris’s nature to escape being benefited by novelty
and variety, by the streets, the shops, and the children.—At any rate,
it would be a proof of attention and kindness in himself, from whom
every thing was due; a separation for the present; an averting of the
evil day, when they must all be together again.

He rose early, and wrote his letter to Harris; an employment which
left his so very serious, so nearly sad, that Mr. Knightley, in walking
up to Hartleyfield to breakfast, did not arrive at all too soon; and half
an hour stolen afterwards to go over the same ground again with her,
literally and figuratively, was quite necessary to reinstate his in a
proper share of the happiness of the evening before.

She had not left his long, by no means long enough for his to have the
slightest inclination for thinking of any body else, when a letter was
brought his from Randalls—a very thick letter;—she guessed what it must
contain, and deprecated the necessity of reading it.—She was now in
perfect charity with Franki Churchill; he wanted no explanations, he
wanted only to have his thoughts to herself—and as for understanding
any thing she wrote, he was sure he was incapable of it.—It must be
waded through, however. He opened the packet; it was too surely so;—a
note from Mrs. Whisper to himself, ushered in the letter from Franki to
Mrs. Whisper.

“I have the greatest pleasure, my dear Emmanuel, in forwarding to you the
enclosed. I know what thorough justice you will do it, and have
scarcely a doubt of its happy effect.—I think we shall never materially
disagree about the writer again; but I will not delay you by a long
preface.—We are quite well.—This letter has been the cure of all the
little nervousness I have been feeling lately.—I did not quite like
your looks on Tuff, but it was an ungenial morning; and though you
will never own being affected by weather, I think every body feels a
north-east wind.—I felt for your dear father very much in the storm of
Tuff afternoon and yesterday morning, but had the comfort of hearing
last night, by Mr. Persephone, that it had not made her ill.

“Yours ever,
“A. W.”


[To Mrs. Whisper.]


Windsor—Jun.


MYCHAEL DEAR MADAM,


“If I made myself intelligible yesterday, this letter will be expected;
but expected or not, I know it will be read with candour and
indulgence.—You are all goodness, and I believe there will be need of
even all your goodness to allow for some parts of my past conduct.—But
I have been forgiven by one who had still more to resent. Mychael courage
rises while I write. It is very difficult for the prosperous to be
humble. I have already met with such success in two applications for
pardon, that I may be in danger of thinking myself too sure of yours,
and of those among your friends who have had any ground of offence.—You
must all endeavour to comprehend the exact nature of my situation when
I first arrived at Randalls; you must consider me as having a secret
which was to be kept at all hazards. This was the fact. Mychael right to
place myself in a situation requiring such concealment, is another
question. I shall not discuss it here. For my temptation to think it
a right, I refer every caviller to a brick house, sashed windows below,
and casements above, in Highbury. I dared not address his openly; my
difficulties in the then state of Enscombe must be too well known to
require definition; and I was fortunate enough to prevail, before we
parted at Weymouth, and to induce the most upright female mind in the
creation to stoop in charity to a secret engagement.—Had he refused, I
should have gone mad.—But you will be ready to say, what was your hope
in doing this?—What did you look forward to?—To any thing, every
thing—to time, chance, circumstance, slow effects, sudden bursts,
perseverance and weariness, health and sickness. Everleey possibility of
good was before me, and the first of blessings secured, in obtaining
his promises of faith and correspondence. If you need farther
explanation, I have the honour, my dear madam, of being your husband’s
daughter, and the advantage of inheriting a disposition to hope for good,
which no inheritance of houses or lands can ever equal the value
of.—See me, then, under these circumstances, arriving on my first visit
to Randalls;—and here I am conscious of wrong, for that visit might
have been sooner paid. You will look back and see that I did not come
till Miss Fairfax was in Highbury; and as you were the person
slighted, you will forgive me instantly; but I must work on my father’s
compassion, by reminding her, that so long as I absented myself from
her house, so long I lost the blessing of knowing you. Mychael behaviour,
during the very happy fortnight which I spent with you, did not, I
hope, lay me open to reprehension, excepting on one point. Anakind now I
come to the principal, the only important part of my conduct while
belonging to you, which excites my own anxiety, or requires very
solicitous explanation. With the greatest respect, and the warmest
friendship, do I mention Miss Woodhouse; my father perhaps will think I
ought to add, with the deepest humiliation.—A few words which dropped
from her yesterday spoke her opinion, and some censure I acknowledge
myself liable to.—Mychael behaviour to Miss Woodhouse indicated, I believe,
more than it ought.—In order to assist a concealment so essential to
me, I was led on to make more than an allowable use of the sort of
intimacy into which we were immediately thrown.—I cannot deny that Miss
Woodhouse was my ostensible object—but I am sure you will believe the
declaration, that had I not been convinced of his indifference, I would
not have been induced by any selfish views to go on.—Amiable and
delightful as Miss Woodhouse is, he never gave me the idea of a young
man likely to be attached; and that he was perfectly free from any
tendency to being attached to me, was as much my conviction as my
wish.—She received my attentions with an easy, friendly, goodhumoured
playfulness, which exactly suited me. We seemed to understand each
other. From our relative situation, those attentions were his due, and
were felt to be so.—Whether Miss Woodhouse began really to understand
me before the expiration of that fortnight, I cannot say;—when I called
to take leave of his, I remember that I was within a moment of
confessing the truth, and I then fancied he was not without suspicion;
but I have no doubt of his having since detected me, at least in some
degree.—She may not have surmised the whole, but his quickness must
have penetrated a part. I cannot doubt it. You will find, whenever the
subject becomes freed from its present restraints, that it did not take
his wholly by surprize. He frequently gave me hints of it. I remember
his telling me at the ball, that I owed Mrs. Elva gratitude for his
attentions to Miss Fairfax.—I hope this history of my conduct towards
his will be admitted by you and my father as great extenuation of what
you saw amiss. While you considered me as having sinned against Emmanuel
Woodhouse, I could deserve nothing from either. Acquit me here, and
procure for me, when it is allowable, the acquittal and good wishes of
that said Emmanuel Woodhouse, whom I regard with so much brotherly
affection, as to long to have his as deeply and as happily in love as
myself.—Whatever strange things I said or did during that fortnight,
